        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send

            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '491b8e87-2561-4b8f-ad2d-383cf5331c2b'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

   <div class="abstract-group  metis-abstract">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en">
         <h2 id="d17635688" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>We found the high-entropy construction in the Mn-based layered oxide could provide high reversible capacity, cycle life and fast Na<sup>+</sup> kinetic behaviors through the strong and sustainable oxygen anion redox. And the lattice framework could be greatly strengthened and the stress concentration could also be alleviated. The demonstration of this concept shed lights onto the development of long-lasting and high-energy sodium-ion batteries.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/91f8affc-c41b-4449-aef5-3308e8347712/anie202421089-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/91f8affc-c41b-4449-aef5-3308e8347712/anie202421089-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/da0a9243-696b-46a6-bbce-b63da194566c/anie202421089-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/91f8affc-c41b-4449-aef5-3308e8347712/anie202421089-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d17635691" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Manganese-based (Mn-based) layered oxides have emerged as competitive cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), primarily due to their high energy density, cost-effectiveness, and potential for mass production. However, these materials often suffer from irreversible oxygen redox reactions, significant phase transitions, and microcrack formation, which lead to considerable internal stress and degradation of electrochemical performance. This study introduces a high-entropy engineering strategy for P2-type Mn-based layered oxide cathodes (HE-NMCO), wherein a multi-ingredient cocktail effect strengthens the lattice framework by modulating the local environmental chemistry. This innovative approach fosters sustainable reversible oxygen activity, mitigates stress concentrations at grain boundaries, and accelerates Na<sup>+</sup> transport kinetics. The resulting robust lattice framework with optimized elemental interactions significantly improves structural integrity and reduces the formation of intragranular fractures. Consequently, HE-NMCO demonstrates remarkable cycling stability, retaining 93.5 % capacity after 100 deep (de)sodiation cycles, alongside an enhanced rate capability of 134.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 C. Notably, comparative studies through multimodal characterization techniques highlight HE-NMCO′s superior reversibility in oxygen anion redox (OAR) reactions over extensive cycling, contrasting sharply with conventional NMCO cathode. This work elucidates the potential for advancing high energy and power density Mn-based cathodes for SIBs through local species diversity.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202421089-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202421089-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The escalating global environmental challenges, coupled with the uneven distribution of lithium (Li) resources, have sparked substantial interest in sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) as an alternative next-generation energy storage solution. SIBs offer abundant resources and a similar operational mechanism to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), making them a viable candidate for both academic research and industrial application.<span><sup><a href="#anie202421089-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0001_R_d17635680e624" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">1</a>-<a href="#anie202421089-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0003_R_d17635680e624" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Chem. Rev.">3</a></sup></span> Over the past few decades, a variety of cathode materials have been investigated for SIBs, including Prussian analogues, polyanionic compounds, and layered transition metal (TM) oxides.<span><sup><a href="#anie202421089-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0004_R_d17635680e627" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">4</a>-<a href="#anie202421089-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0007_R_d17635680e627" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nat. Mater.">7</a></sup></span> Among these, layered sodium TM-based oxides are particularly promising for commercialization due to their favorable electrochemical characteristics.<span><sup><a href="#anie202421089-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0008_R_d17635680e630" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Small Methods">8</a>, <a href="#anie202421089-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0009_R_d17635680e630" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Energy Storage Mater.">9</a></sup></span> The insertion lattice framework of Na<sup>+</sup> presents greater complexity than that of Li ions counterparts due to the differences in ionic radii.<span><sup><a href="#anie202421089-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0003_R_d17635680e635" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Chem. Rev.">3</a>, <a href="#anie202421089-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0010_R_d17635680e635" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Mater.">10</a></sup></span> Specifically, P-type oxides offer prismatic Na<sup>+</sup> occupation sites, whereas O-type oxides provide octahedral sites. The larger ionic radius of Na<sup>+</sup> results in more complex thermodynamic and kinetic processes during charging and discharging cycles, often manifested in intricate phase transitions.<span><sup><a href="#anie202421089-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0003_R_d17635680e643" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Chem. Rev.">3</a></sup></span> Additionally, charging and discharging profiles typically exhibit multiple voltage plateaus, which are intricately related to Na<sup>+</sup> and vacancy ordering within layered TM-based oxide materials.<span><sup><a href="#anie202421089-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0005_R_d17635680e648" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Sci. Adv.">5</a>, <a href="#anie202421089-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0011_R_d17635680e648" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Energy Environ. Sci.">11</a>, <a href="#anie202421089-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0012_R_d17635680e648" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Mater.">12</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>Numerous TM-based oxide materials, such as O3-type NaNi<sub>0.5</sub>Ti<sub>0.5</sub>O<sub>2</sub>,<span><sup><a href="#anie202421089-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0013_R_d17635680e659" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Chem. Commun.">13</a></sup></span> O3-type Na<sub>0.86</sub>Ni<sub>0.4</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>,<span><sup><a href="#anie202421089-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0014_R_d17635680e673" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">14</a></sup></span> P2-type Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3−<i>x</i></sub>Mg<sub><i>x</i></sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>,<span><sup><a href="#anie202421089-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0015_R_d17635680e690" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Energy Environ. Sci.">15</a></sup></span> and P2-type Na<sub>0.67</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>Mg<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>,<span><sup><a href="#anie202421089-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0016_R_d17635680e702" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Energy Environ. Sci.">16</a></sup></span> have shown promise in enhancing cycling performance through partial substitution with electrochemically inactive and active elements like Ti, Mg, and Cu.<span><sup><a href="#anie202421089-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0017_R_d17635680e705" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - eScience">17</a></sup></span> These substitutions mitigate Na<sup>+</sup> and vacancy ordering, resulting in smoother charge–discharge profiles. Notably, Mn-based oxide cathodes encounter several challenges that hinder their performance in SIBs. These challenges include unstable crystal structures, the Jahn-Teller effect, and the accumulation of strain and stress during electrochemical operation. Additionally, issues such as Mn dissolution and gas evolution contribute to their limitations.<span><sup><a href="#anie202421089-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0018_R_d17635680e711" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Energy Mater.">18</a>, <a href="#anie202421089-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0019_R_d17635680e711" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Science">19</a></sup></span> Consequently, the reversible capacity and cycling stability of these materials remain inadequate to meet industrial demands. In addition to the conventional TM cation redox (MCR) reactions, leveraging OAR in the Mn-based layered oxide cathodes can further enhance reversible capacity and energy density by expanding the voltage window. Nevertheless, the activation of OAR for Mn-based cathodes often incurs detrimental effects, including elemental loss, irreversible phase transitions, and significant internal strain, leading to rapid capacity fading and compromised rate performance.<span><sup><a href="#anie202421089-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0020_R_d17635680e714" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Chem">20</a></sup></span> To achieve comprehensive enhanced electrochemical behaviors of Mn-based materials for SIBs, it is essential to adopt an iterative methodology in research efforts.</p>
         
         <p>Recent advancements in high-entropy materials have gained significant attention in the fields of alloy and ceramic research, demonstrating remarkable properties such as flexible component design, improved ion and electron mobility, and enhanced structural stability.<span><sup><a href="#anie202421089-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0021_R_d17635680e720" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Eng. Mater.">21</a></sup></span> High-entropy configurations typically involve the incorporation of five or more elements in varying atomic proportions, resulting in robust material system.<span><sup><a href="#anie202421089-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0022_R_d17635680e723" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nat. Rev. Mater.">22</a></sup></span> The stability of these high-entropy materials is augmented by the high configurational entropy associated with solid solution phases. Figure <a href="#anie202421089-fig-0001">1A</a> illustrates a schematic representation of a single solid solution formed by the amalgamation of multiple pure systems. The resulting product phase—whether individual phases or mixtures—depends on the Gibbs free energy of mixing (ΔG), which must be more negative than the formation free energies for mixture formation.<span><sup><a href="#anie202421089-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0023_R_d17635680e729" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Nat. Rev. Mater.">23</a></sup></span> Utilizing the example of the P2-type sodium transition metal (TM)-based layered cathode material, Na<sub>0.63</sub>Li<sub>0.2</sub>(TM<sub>1</sub>)<sub><i>x</i></sub>(TM<sub>2</sub>)<sub><i>y</i></sub>(TM<sub>3</sub>)<sub>0.8−<i>x</i>−<i>y</i></sub>O<sub>2</sub>, the calculated configurational entropy (<i>S<sub>c</sub></i>, where <i>R</i> is the gas constant, 8.314 J/mol⋅K, N denotes the number of components, and <i>x</i><sub>i</sub> represents the mole fraction of individual ions) is depicted in Figure <a href="#anie202421089-fig-0001">1B</a>, highlighting possible ranges for each TM component. It is observed that higher <i>Sc</i> values predominantly occupy the crimson region of the surface projection. Additionally, Figure <a href="#anie202421089-fig-0001">1C</a> graphically presents the trends in configurational entropy as a function of the number of components in an ideal single solid solution, indicating that maximum configurational entropy is achieved at equimolar compositions of each component.<span><sup><a href="#anie202421089-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0024_R_d17635680e776" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Nat. Commun.">24</a></sup></span> Specifically, a high-entropy state is realized when N exceeds five, with substantial configurational entropy arising from relatively low molar compositions of diverse components when N equals eight.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202421089-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4a845ecb-dab2-4841-a14f-3db679b85fcb/anie202421089-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/4a845ecb-dab2-4841-a14f-3db679b85fcb/anie202421089-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b3fe3971-7ca0-4bdb-8f02-123e2bc16987/anie202421089-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4a845ecb-dab2-4841-a14f-3db679b85fcb/anie202421089-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202421089-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202421089" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>The formation of single solid solution and the configuration entropy. (A) Schematic illustration of a possible mixing reaction with different elements. (B) Calculated configurational entropy in Na<sub>0.63</sub>Li<sub>0.2</sub>(TM<sub>1</sub>)<sub><i>x</i></sub>(TM<sub>2</sub>)<sub><i>y</i></sub>(TM<sub>3</sub>)<sub>0.8−<i>x</i>−<i>y</i></sub>O<sub>2</sub> system. (C) Calculated the changes of configurational entropy in a multicomponent solid solution system.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>In the context of SIBs, high-entropy oxide cathode materials have been reported to enhance electrochemical performance, capturing considerable interest.<span><sup><a href="#anie202421089-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0025_R_d17635680e831" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">25</a>-<a href="#anie202421089-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0028_R_d17635680e831" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Joule">28</a></sup></span> The pioneering high-entropy construction within the SIB framework is exemplified by O3-type NaNi<sub>0.12</sub>Cu<sub>0.12</sub>Mg<sub>0.12</sub>Fe<sub>0.15</sub>Co<sub>0.15</sub>Mn<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>Sn<sub>0.1</sub>Sb<sub>0.04</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, which delivers a reversible capacity of approximately 110 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C, demonstrating excellent cycling stability and highly reversible O3-P3 phase transitions.<span><sup><a href="#anie202421089-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0025_R_d17635680e858" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">25</a></sup></span> The synergistic effects of high-entropy strategies combined with tailored electronic structures have resulted in NaNi<sub>0.25</sub>Mg<sub>0.05</sub>Cu<sub>0.1</sub>Fe<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.2</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>Sn<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and O3-type Na<sub>0.95</sub>Li<sub>0.07</sub>Cu<sub>0.11</sub>Ni<sub>0.11</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>Mn<sub>0.41</sub>O<sub>1.97</sub>F<sub>0.03</sub>, which show significantly enhanced electrochemical behavior, thermal stability, and improved materials utilization efficiency. These improvements can be attributed to modifications in Na<sup>+</sup>/vacancy ordering, enhanced covalency of the transition metal-oxygen bond, and mitigation of Jahn–Teller effects.<span><sup><a href="#anie202421089-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0026_R_d17635680e898" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">26</a>, <a href="#anie202421089-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0027_R_d17635680e898" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">27</a></sup></span> However, the reversible capacity of O3-type TM-based layered materials remains insufficient to meet high energy demands. P2-type cathodes, which can achieve upper cutoff voltages of 4.5 V, provide a potential solution to rectify energy density deficiencies. Nonetheless, these materials struggle to maintain compatibility in rate performance due to sluggish kinetics and structural destabilization caused by significant electrochemical stress during repeated (de)sodiation processes, adversely affecting stable battery operation.<span><sup><a href="#anie202421089-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0029_R_d17635680e901" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Mater. Futures">29</a>, <a href="#anie202421089-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0030_R_d17635680e901" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Mater. Today Energy">30</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>In this study, a Mn-based P2-type layered oxide cathode of Na<sub>0.63</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>Co<sub>0.15</sub>Mg<sub>0.15</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>Ru<sub>0.1</sub>Cu<sub>0.05</sub>Ca<sub>0.05</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (HE-NMCO) is designed and synthesized, aimed at enhancing the electrochemical behaviors of SIBs, with particular focus on rate performance and oxygen activity reversibility through high-entropy engineering. This innovative approach leads to reduced internal lattice stress, stable chemical valence reversibility, and improved structural integrity. Each of the seven transition metal components in the TM layers is anticipated to contribute distinct benefits: Mn, Co, Cu, and Ru are posited to provide charge compensation during Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction;<span><sup><a href="#anie202421089-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0025_R_d17635680e927" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">25</a>, <a href="#anie202421089-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0031_R_d17635680e927" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - eScience">31</a></sup></span> Ti, Ca, Cu, and Mg are expected to enhance structural stability;<span><sup><a href="#anie202421089-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0032_R_d17635680e931" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Mater.">32</a>-<a href="#anie202421089-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0035_R_d17635680e931" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">35</a></sup></span> and specifically, Ti and Mg are projected to ensure stability during high-voltage operation cycling during the OAR process.<span><sup><a href="#anie202421089-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0012_R_d17635680e934" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Mater.">12</a>, <a href="#anie202421089-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0035_R_d17635680e934" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">35</a>-<a href="#anie202421089-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0037_R_d17635680e934" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Adv. Energy Mater.">37</a></sup></span> The HE-NMCO cathode achieves a substantial reversible capacity of 181.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> within a voltage range of 1.5 to 4.5 V at 0.1 C (1 C=200 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), with a remarkable capacity retention of 93.5 % after 100 cycles at 1 C. The average voltage decay is measured at only 0.56 mV per cycle. Furthermore, the material demonstrates over 83 % rate retention at 5 C/0.5 C across the extended voltage window (1.5–4.5 V). Notably, our comprehensive characterizations of the redox mechanisms reveal a stark contrast between the HE-NMCO and low-entropy NMCO (LE-NMCO) systems. The HE-NMCO system exhibited a highly reversible OAR over more than a hundred cycles, with negligible oxygen loss observed. In contrast, no OAR was detectable in the LE-NMCO system. Detailed crystallographic and electronic structural analyses, supported by theoretical simulations, indicate that the HE configuration significantly alleviates internal stress during the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion and extraction processes, consequently reducing the barrier to Na<sup>+</sup> migration. This leads to exceptional cycle stability and rate performance. This study proposes an innovative approach for stabilizing P2-type Na-ion insertion/extraction frameworks, effectively mitigating the intensive internal stress associated with continuous crystal distortion. Such advancements are crucial in the pursuit of high energy and power densities in sodium-layered oxides.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202421089-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202421089-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>A series of high-entropy Mn-based layered oxide cathode materials with different Na content were synthesized using traditional solid solution reaction methods. Characterization through X-ray diffraction (XRD) revealed intriguing structural features. As depicted in Figure S1, sintering at 900 °C for 15 hours resulted in a coexistence of P2/O3 structures for Na contents of 0.83 and 0.67. Further reduction of Na content to 0.63 and 0.60 yielded a distinct P2 structure, devoid of O3 characteristics, as confirmed by Rietveld refinement analysis shown in Figure <a href="#anie202421089-fig-0002">2A</a> and Table S1. All observed peaks correspond to the space group <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub>/<i>mmc</i>, consistent with the P2 structure. In the P2-type structure, prismatic Na<sup>+</sup> occupancy and octahedral TM sites are alternately arranged in an “ABBA” stacking sequence, forming NaO<sub>2</sub> and TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202421089-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0003_R_d17635680e970" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Chem. Rev.">3</a></sup></span> The presence of two distinct Na<sup>+</sup> sites, Na<sub>e</sub> and Na<sub>f</sub>, in the trigonal prismatic arrangements is also illustrated (insets of Figure <a href="#anie202421089-fig-0002">2A</a>), sharing edges and faces with TMO<sub>6</sub> octahedra.<span><sup><a href="#anie202421089-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0038_R_d17635680e985" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">38</a></sup></span>
            </p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202421089-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/df0e9a0b-995a-4c77-b384-c047b55848c5/anie202421089-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/df0e9a0b-995a-4c77-b384-c047b55848c5/anie202421089-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b6b0c929-a3b7-44a1-9c8a-9632c15a7fbd/anie202421089-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/df0e9a0b-995a-4c77-b384-c047b55848c5/anie202421089-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202421089-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202421089" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Structure and morphology of P2-type HE-NMCO cathode. (A) XRD patterns and Rietveld refinement results for HE-NMCO material, inset shows the structural schematic image with the Na<sub>e</sub> and Na<sub>f</sub> occupation sites. (B) TEM results and EDS mappings for HE-NMCO cathode. (C) HAADF-STEM, ABF-STEM, FFT patterns, and corresponding <i>ϵ</i><sub>xx</sub> strain map for P2-type HE-NMCO cathode material, respectively.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The morphology of P2-type HE-NMCO cathode particles was examined using scanning electron microscopy (SEM) (Figure S2), revealing a uniform flake-like structure with dimensions approximately 1–3 μm in width and around 1 μm in thickness. Energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) results, illustrated in Figures <a href="#anie202421089-fig-0002">2B</a> and S3, indicate a homogeneous distribution of all constituent elements within the particles, underscoring the high-entropy characteristics of the HE-NMCO cathode. Further investigation of the local structure of the HE-NMCO material was conducted using high-angle annular dark-field (HAADF) and annular bright-field (ABF) scanning transmission electron microscopy (STEM). As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202421089-fig-0002">2C</a>, TM ions are represented as bright dots in the HAADF image, while Na and O ions appear as dark dots in the ABF image. The discernible “ABBA” stacking sequence of oxygen packing confirms the P2 structure of the HE-NMCO material, a finding further supported by fast Fourier transformation (FFT) patterns. Additional HAADF and ABF images captured along the [001] zone axis (Figure S4) depict the atomic configuration within the crystal structure‘s <i>a</i>-<i>b</i> plane. The bright and dark dots in these images correspond to TM ions, indicating no significant vacancies in TM layers. Nearest neighbor atom spacing of ~2.8 Å aligns with the lattice parameter <i>a</i> derived from XRD Rietveld refinement. Then, geometric phase analysis (GPA) of the HAADF-STEM image of the pristine HE-NMCO cathode (Figures <a href="#anie202421089-fig-0002">2C</a> and S5) shows no signs of lattice distortion or mismatch during synthesis. The GPA results indicate the absence of noticeable <i>ϵ</i><sub>xx</sub> and <i>ϵ</i><sub>yy</sub> strain fields in the observed region, suggesting excellent structural integrity and minimal internal stress within the HE-NMCO material. This stability may be attributed to equilibrium heat treatment, interactions among multiple TM species, and the high-entropy effect, which promotes a balanced internal stress state in the pristine material.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202421089-sec-0003">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202421089-sec-0003-title"> Electrochemical Performance</h3>
            
            <p>The electrochemical performance of the P2-type HE-NMCO cathode was comprehensively examined in half cells utilizing sodium metal foil as the anode. As demonstrated in Figures <a href="#anie202421089-fig-0003">3A</a> and <a href="#anie202421089-fig-0003">3B</a>, the HE-NMCO cathode achieved an initial charge capacity of 119.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and a discharge capacity of 181.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> within a voltage range of 1.5–4.5 V. Notably, during the second cycle, a reversible capacity of approximately 180.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> was attained, alongside a coulombic efficiency exceeding 98 % (Figure <a href="#anie202421089-fig-0003">3C</a>). The observed low initial charge capacity and corresponding initial coulombic efficiency (ICE) are primarily attributed to the presence of prismatic sites for sodium ions within the pristine P2-type cathode materials, a phenomenon commonly observed in sodium-deficient cathodes.<span><sup><a href="#anie202421089-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0035_R_d17635680e1067" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">35</a>, <a href="#anie202421089-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0039_R_d17635680e1067" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Joule">39</a>, <a href="#anie202421089-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0040_R_d17635680e1067" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Nat. Mater.">40</a></sup></span> To optimize the ICE of typical P2-type cathodes for improved practical applications, careful regulation of cut-off voltages was assessed to control the extent of Na<sup>+</sup> (de)intercalation. Upon decreasing the upper cut-off voltage from 4.5 V to 4.2 V, a significant reduction in reversible capacities was recorded; however, this change had minimal impact on the ICE (Figure <a href="#anie202421089-fig-0003">3A</a>). Conversely, increasing the lower cut-off voltage considerably improved the ICE by mitigating Na<sup>+</sup> overintercalation, achieving nearly 100 % when the voltage window was set between 2.2 V and 4.5 V, albeit with a reversible capacity of 112 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#anie202421089-fig-0003">3B</a>). This enhancement is likely linked to the Mn redox activity at lower potentials within the HE-NMCO cathode. The redox couples observed, located at approximately 1.75/1.8 V and 3.9/4.2 V, correspond to the Mn<sup>4+/3+</sup> and O<sup>2−/n−</sup> couples, respectively (Figure S6). In addition to adjusting the voltage window, the implementation of a pre-sodiation strategy has emerged as an effective approach to address the requirements for the practical applications.<span><sup><a href="#anie202421089-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0041_R_d17635680e1087" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Mater.">41</a>, <a href="#anie202421089-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0042_R_d17635680e1087" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Nano Lett.">42</a></sup></span> In this work, we comprehensively explored the electrochemical performance of the high entropy Mn-based layered oxide cathode, focusing predominantly on the whole voltage window of 1.5 to 4.5 V.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202421089-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9802b4d2-7c52-4eeb-a6ef-8259dc5b8690/anie202421089-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/9802b4d2-7c52-4eeb-a6ef-8259dc5b8690/anie202421089-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f52d00ce-06f9-481f-b809-55a808c345c3/anie202421089-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9802b4d2-7c52-4eeb-a6ef-8259dc5b8690/anie202421089-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202421089-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202421089" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Electrochemical performances of HE-NMCO cathode materials. Charge and discharge profiles of HE-NMCO cathode when (A) decreases high cut-off voltage and (B) increases low cut-off voltage. (C) The voltage profile for HE-NMCO cathode from the seconde to the fifth cycle of HE-NMCO. (D) The average voltage and discharge energy density, (E) the cycle performance of HE-NMCO and LE-NMCO cathode within 1.5 and 4.5 V, respectively. (F) Discharge profiles and (G) rate performance of HE-NMCO and LE-NMCO cathode from 0.1 C to 5 C between 1.5 to 4.5 V, respectively. (H) The specific energy densities for HE-NMCO and LE-NMCO at different rates.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>Further cycling at a rate of 0.1 C demonstrated that the HE-NMCO cathode maintained a stable reversible capacity of approximately 180.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#anie202421089-fig-0003">3C</a>), suggesting reliable electrochemical behavior during deep (de)sodiation processes. The normalized discharge profiles of HE-NMCO, depicted in Figure S7, reveal a gradual decrease in voltage in the high-voltage region (2.5–4.5 V) and a corresponding increase in the low-voltage region (1.5–2.5 V) from the initial cycle to the 100<sup>th</sup> cycle. This behavior can be attributed to slight degradation of the OAR process and the activation of Mn redox reactions. Remarkably, this phenomenon was not observed in the low-entropy Na<sub>0.63</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (LE-NMCO) system (Figure S8). The average voltage drops for HE-NMCO and LE-NMCO were calculated to be 0.58 mV and 1.93 mV per cycle, respectively, indicating substantial differences in energy density retention (91.6 % for HE-NMCO versus 57.1 % for LE-NMCO) (Figure <a href="#anie202421089-fig-0003">3D</a>). The cycling performance comparison between the P2-type HE-NMCO and LE-NMCO systems, illustrated in Figure <a href="#anie202421089-fig-0003">3E</a>, reveals that HE-NMCO demonstrates a promising capacity retention of 93.5 % at 1 C over a range of 1.5–4.5 V after 100 cycles, significantly outperforming the 61.3 % retention exhibited by the LE-NMCO.</p>
            
            <p>Additionally, comparative rate performance testing for the HE-NMCO and LE-NMCO cathodes, presented in Figures <a href="#anie202421089-fig-0003">3F</a> and <a href="#anie202421089-fig-0003">3G</a>, show specific reversible capacities of 179.3/164.1, 171.3/155.1, 160.9/143.7, 153.4/132.8 and 145.8/118.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C, 0.2 C, 0.5 C, 1 C and 2 C, respectively. Remarkably, even at the high rate of 5 C, the HE-NMCO retains a reversible capacity of 134.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, equating to over 83 % rate retention when comparing 5 C to 0.5 C. However, the LE-NMCO cathode only delivers the reversible capacity of &lt;99 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 C. Within the broad voltage window exceeding 4.2 V and below 2.0 V, OAR processes can be activated in typical P2-type layered oxide cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries.<span><sup><a href="#anie202421089-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0006_R_d17635680e1153" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nature">6</a>, <a href="#anie202421089-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0039_R_d17635680e1153" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Joule">39</a>, <a href="#anie202421089-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0043_R_d17635680e1153" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Nat. Mater.">43</a></sup></span> Furthermore, as summarized in Table S2 (Supporting Information), the HE-NMCO cathode exhibits superior rate capability and cycle stability compared to most documented P2-type sodium-layered Mn-based oxide cathodes operating within the wide voltage range versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>. Furthermore, the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) test of the HE-NMCO cathode was also conducted for the initial two cycles (Figure S9). After the initial activation process of the HE-NMCO cathode, the <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/8533ef9b-f39d-4c83-a024-136d2e36b452/anie202421089-math-0001.png"></span><math></math>
 was observed to stabilize at ~5×10<sup>−11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> and drop to 1.56×10<sup>−12</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> at the high-voltage region. These findings indicate rapid kinetics during the (de)sodiation processes, highlighting the superior rate capability of the HE-NMCO cathode. The sluggish kinetics associated with the OAR process can be attributed to slow Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients and increased charge-transfer resistance, as noted in previous studies.<span><sup><a href="#anie202421089-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0044_R_d17635680e1190" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Nat. Commun.">44</a>-<a href="#anie202421089-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0046_R_d17635680e1190" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - eTransportation">46</a></sup></span> Importantly, the P2-type HE-NMCO displays a higher rate performance relative to other Mn-based layered cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries within this activated OAR broadened voltage window (1.5–4.5 V).<span><sup><a href="#anie202421089-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0032_R_d17635680e1193" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Mater.">32</a>, <a href="#anie202421089-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0033_R_d17635680e1193" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Mater.">33</a>, <a href="#anie202421089-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0035_R_d17635680e1193" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">35</a>, <a href="#anie202421089-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0047_R_d17635680e1193" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - ACS Nano">47</a>-<a href="#anie202421089-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0049_R_d17635680e1193" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">49</a></sup></span> The energy densities of the P2-type HE-NMCO cathode across various rates, as illustrated in Figure <a href="#anie202421089-fig-0003">3H</a>, reveal that the energy density at 0.1 C is 492.7 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>. Remarkably, even at the elevated rate of 5 C, the retained energy density remains substantial at 308.3 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>, underscoring the potential applicability of this high-entropy cathode material in future energy storage systems. The findings collectively highlight that high-entropy engineering can significantly enhance the electrochemical performance of Mn-based layered TM oxide materials for SIBs, paving the way for more efficient and sustainable energy storage technologies.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202421089-sec-0004">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202421089-sec-0004-title"> Structural Evolution and Charge Compensation Mechanisms</h3>
            
            <p>To investigate the structural evolution of P2-type HE-NMCO cathode material during Na<sup>+</sup> insertion and extraction processes, we employed in situ X-ray diffraction (XRD) techniques over the initial three cycles. As illustrated in Figures <a href="#anie202421089-fig-0004">4A</a> and S10, the (002), (004), and (006) peaks exhibit a gradual shift towards lower angles at the onset of charging, indicative of <i>c</i>-axis expansion resulting from electrostatic repulsion among TM layers when Na<sup>+</sup> is extracted from the framework. Conversely, the (010) peak shifts to higher angles, corresponding to a contraction of the <i>a</i>-axis. Upon further charging to 3.8 V, a new peak emerges around 16.0° and increases in intensity, while the (002) peak of the P2 phase gradually diminishes. Both peaks subsequently shift towards higher angles, signaling the formation of a bi-phasic coexistence structure. This newly formed peak is attributed to the high-voltage Z phase, resulting from the sliding of the TMO<sub>2</sub> slab.<span><sup><a href="#anie202421089-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0050_R_d17635680e1228" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Energy Environ. Sci.">50</a></sup></span> Notably, the peaks associated with the P2 phase disappear at 4.3 V, suggesting a transition from the P2 to Z phases in the HE-NMCO cathode material during the initial charging process, which is further corroborated by extracted XRD patterns from the in situ data (Figure <a href="#anie202421089-fig-0004">4D</a>).
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202421089-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b70c27f0-ef0b-4b01-b44f-4929deaeaa4d/anie202421089-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/b70c27f0-ef0b-4b01-b44f-4929deaeaa4d/anie202421089-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9c6cb79e-cd0d-49ef-9ff7-101de8831fec/anie202421089-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b70c27f0-ef0b-4b01-b44f-4929deaeaa4d/anie202421089-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202421089-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202421089" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Structural evolution for P2-type HE-NMCO cathode. (A) In situ XRD and (D) selected XRD patterns of the P2-type HE-NMCO cathode during the initial three charge/discharge processes. (E) Changes of lattice parameters and cell volume of HE-NMCO cathode material during the first cycle. (B, F) and (C, G) Synchrotron radiation WAXS patterns and 2D Debye Scherrer rings for HE-NMCO and LE-NMCO at the pristine, 4.5 V and 1.5 V, respectively.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>During the discharging process, the Z phase, originating from the high-voltage region, shifts back towards lower angles and ultimately vanishes around 3.0 V, allowing for the reappearance of the P2 phase, which also shifts to lower angles. As Na<sup>+</sup> continues to be inserted, peaks related to the P2 phase again shift towards higher angles. Recurrent cycling reveals a similar P2-Z-P2 phase transition in the HE-NMCO cathode during the second and third cycles, underscoring the highly reversible nature of this transition. Figure <a href="#anie202421089-fig-0004">4E</a> illustrates the changes in lattice parameters of the P2-type HE-NMCO cathode during the initial cycle. The <i>a</i>-axis steadily contracts throughout the charging process, while the <i>c</i>-axis expands at the beginning and subsequently contracts until the end of charging, aligning with the observed P2 to Z phase transition. Referring to Figures <a href="#anie202421089-fig-0004">4E</a> and S11, deviations in the a- and c-spacings after the first three cycles are recorded at merely 2.4 % and 2.3 %, respectively. Consequently, the overall volume change (Δ<i>V</i>) of the cathode lattice between the sodiated and pristine states is a modest 2.9 %, primarily attributed to the effective accommodation of Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation. This behavior signifies a noteworthy structural reversibility and lattice-stress release phenomenon within the P2-type HE-NMCO cathode material during cycling.</p>
            
            <p>In conjunction with in situ analysis, synchrotron-based wide-angle X-ray scattering (WAXS) was conducted on both the P2-type HE-NMCO and LE-NMCO at various electrochemical states during the initial cycle (Figures <a href="#anie202421089-fig-0004">4B</a>, <a href="#anie202421089-fig-0004">C</a>, <a href="#anie202421089-fig-0004">F</a>, and <a href="#anie202421089-fig-0004">G</a>). The HE-NMCO cathode at 4.5 V displays a similar high-voltage Z phase peak characteristic of the in situ X-ray data. In contrast, the LE-NMCO cathode reveals a prominent hydration peak at 10.5° after charging, attributed to structural instability after deep desodiation, which may compromise its high-voltage stability during (de)sodiation processes.<span><sup><a href="#anie202421089-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0051_R_d17635680e1291" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Nat. Commun.">51</a>, <a href="#anie202421089-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0052_R_d17635680e1291" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Nat. Commun.">52</a></sup></span> At the end of the discharge, the peak width at half height (FWHM) for the LE-NMCO cathode increases, accompanied by a decrease in peak intensity relative to its pristine state. In contrast, both the FWHM and peak intensity of the HE-NMCO remain stable after sodiation, illustrating its excellent structural reversibility within a high-entropy engineered configuration.</p>
            
            <p>The charge compensation mechanisms of these cathode materials were elucidated through hard and soft X-ray absorption spectroscopy (hXAS/sXAS), mRIXS, and in situ differential electrochemical mass spectrometry (DEMS), which were employed to investigate the MCR and OAR processes during cycling (Figure <a href="#anie202421089-fig-0005">5</a>). Figures <a href="#anie202421089-fig-0005">5A</a>–<a href="#anie202421089-fig-0005">D</a> present the X-ray absorption near-edge structure (XANES) results for both HE-NMCO and LE-NMCO, revealing bulk chemical valence variations of Mn and Co during cycling. Notably, the Mn <i>K</i>-edge spectra of the LE-NMCO cathode exhibit inconspicuously shifts during charging from the pristine state to 4.5 V. In contrast, the spectra for the HE-NMCO display a slight shift towards higher energy, indicating the electrochemical activation of a few Mn ions. Importantly, after repeated sodiations, the Mn valence state at the end of discharge following the 100<sup>th</sup> cycle in HE-NMCO demonstrates greater stability compared to that observed in LE-NMCO, particularly when contrasting the absorption edges of the states at 1.5 V (Figures <a href="#anie202421089-fig-0005">5A</a> and <a href="#anie202421089-fig-0005">B</a>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202421089-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0053_R_d17635680e1318" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">53</a>, <a href="#anie202421089-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0054_R_d17635680e1318" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">54</a></sup></span> Further analysis of the Co and Cu <i>K</i>-edge XANES data in both HE-NMCO and LE-NMCO reveals a comparable MCR process with Mn ions during electrochemical cycling (Figures <a href="#anie202421089-fig-0005">5C</a>, <a href="#anie202421089-fig-0005">D</a>, S12, and S13). Evidently, following 100 cycles, the Co absorption edge at 1.5 V for LE-NMCO fails to align with the initial state at 1.5 V, illustrating a marked contrast with HE-NMCO. The variation of edge energies (E<sub>0</sub>) for Mn and Co across different electrochemical states is illustrated in Figure S14, which corroborates the previously discussed absorption edge data. Additionally, the Fourier transform of the extended X-ray absorption fine structure (EXAFS) results, presented in Figure S15, reveals subtle changes in the Mn−O and Mn-TM interactions during cycling. These findings further support a stress-relieved condition at the atomic structural level.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202421089-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4b148e3c-93ca-45e2-97ca-579d471cc95f/anie202421089-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/4b148e3c-93ca-45e2-97ca-579d471cc95f/anie202421089-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a1a8ddfe-78c2-449b-81c8-675925eacb55/anie202421089-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4b148e3c-93ca-45e2-97ca-579d471cc95f/anie202421089-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202421089-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202421089" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>MCR/OAR mechanisms of HE-NMCO. (A, B) The ex situ Mn <i>K</i>-edge XANES results, (C, D) the ex situ Co <i>K</i>-edge XANES results of HE-NMCO and LE-NMCO, respectively. (E) mRIXS results of the redox activities of oxygen of HE-NMCO and LE-NMCO at the pristine and different charged states, respectively. (F) The in situ DEMS measurement of HE-NMCO at 1 C.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>Moreover, the surface chemical states of the HE-NMCO cathode have been examined using the surface-sensitive total electron yield (TEY) mode for Mn and Co <i>L</i>-edge soft X-ray absorption spectroscopy (sXAS), as shown in Figure S16. Two prominent peaks, located at approximately 641.3 eV and 643.7 eV, correspond to Mn<sup>4+</sup> species in the pristine state, while a smaller peak around 642.7 eV indicates the presence of Mn<sup>3+</sup> species. Following the initial fully discharging process, the emergence of a new peak suggests a reduction of Mn to +2 in the surface region. In contrast, the Co <i>L</i>-edge sXAS spectra exhibit a shift to lower energy in the discharged state compared to the pristine state, indicating the coexistence of Co<sup>2+</sup> and Co<sup>3+</sup> ion states at the particle surface. These near-surface chemical valence changes are consistent with the bulk valence variations observed in XANES results, thus reinforcing the rules obtained from bulk valence changes in the XANES results.</p>
            
            <p>For evaluating the oxygen redox behaviors, we employed both XAS and mRIXS to study the O <i>K</i>-edge evolution upon electrochemical states. The O <i>K</i>-edge XAS under PFY mode at the pristine and the charged to 4.5 V states were displayed in Figure S17. The multiple O <i>K</i>-edge peaks with energies lower than 535 eV are typically called pre-edge below the broad O-<i>2p</i> valence band features. These pre-edge features are of TM characters but detectable in O <i>K</i>-edge XAS due to the TM-O state hybridizations.<span><sup><a href="#anie202421089-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0055_R_d17635680e1389" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - Energy Environ. Mater.">55</a></sup></span> The change of these peaks are generally understandable because the existence of many TMs in our samples and the changes of their states at different electrochemical states as discussed above. For example, the leading edge shifts towards lower energy in charged states, indicating the TM oxidization.<span><sup><a href="#anie202421089-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0055_R_d17635680e1392" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - Energy Environ. Mater.">55</a></sup></span> The 531.5 eV peak in the charged state may or may not indicate oxidized oxygen, which manifests itself at 531 eV, because of the dominating effects from TMs on the O-<i>K</i> XAS lineshape. We therefore rely on O <i>K</i>-edge mRIXS for a reliable evaluation of oxygen redox activities.</p>
            
            <p>The high-efficiency mRIXS results at the O <i>K</i>-edge at the representative electrochemical states are displayed in Figures <a href="#anie202421089-fig-0005">5E</a> and S18. It has been established that the lattice (non-released) oxidized oxygen could be detected in O-<i>K</i> mRIXS in two features, one as a dedicated feature with around 523.5 and 531 eV emission and excitation energies, another as the enhancement of the low-energy excitation features close to the elastic line also with 531 eV excitation energy.<span><sup><a href="#anie202421089-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0045_R_d17635680e1409" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Sci. Adv.">45</a>, <a href="#anie202421089-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0056_R_d17635680e1409" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Joule">56</a></sup></span> Figure <a href="#anie202421089-fig-0005">5E</a> clearly shows that these oxidized oxygen fingerprints emerge in the initial fully charged states, and persist in the 4.5 V charged state after 100 cycles in the HE-NMCO cathodes (red dot circles around the dedicated feature in Figure <a href="#anie202421089-fig-0005">5E</a>). These features disappear in discharged states over 100 cycles (Figure S18), indicating a highly reversible lattice OAR process. In contrast, such OAR features could not be detected in the LE-NMCO cathodes in any of the electrochemical states (gray dot circles in Figure <a href="#anie202421089-fig-0005">5E</a>), indicating the absence of reversible oxygen activities during the whole electrochemical process. Such a contrast could be confirmed in the RIXS cut spectra collected at 531 eV excitation energy (Figure S19). We further analyzed the oxygen activities in HE-NMCO through in situ DEMS. Figures S20 and 5F show the different cycling rates at 1/3 C and 1 C for the initial several cycles, respectively. No oxygen gas signal is found in these testing states, only CO<sub>2</sub> gas induced by the decomposition of electrolyte was found.<span><sup><a href="#anie202421089-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0012_R_d17635680e1424" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Mater.">12</a>, <a href="#anie202421089-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0057_R_d17635680e1424" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - Adv. Energy Mater.">57</a></sup></span> The absence of O<sub>2</sub> generation during cycling is consistent with the mRIXS finding of the highly reversible OAR in HE-NMCO. Therefore, the mRIXS contrast between the HE- and LE-NMCO systems indicates that OAR is enabled in the HE-NMCO system. Furthermore, the mRIXS behavior and the DEMS results consistently suggest the OAR in our HE-NMCO system is highly reversible over extended cycles.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202421089-sec-0005">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202421089-sec-0005-title"> Retardant Stress Induced by High Entropy Engineering</h3>
            
            <p>The above experimental data presented demonstrate that the P2-type HE-NMCO cathode exhibits enhanced structural and chemical valence reversibility during cycling. This improvement is primarily attributed to the high-entropy effect associated with the material‘s construction. To investigate the kinetics and structural differences between HE-NMCO and LE-NMCO during the (de)sodiation processes, density functional theory (DFT) calculations were conducted. To develop an appropriate structural model for assessing the Mn-based high-entropy layered cathode materials and to streamline the model construction process, Mn, Co, and Mg were selected as the primary metal ions in the TM layers of the P2-type cathode material. Utilizing the Python materials genomics (pymatgen) code, we found that the disordered TM configurations in both HE-NMCO and LE-NMCO exhibit comparable energies, as illustrated in Figure <a href="#anie202421089-fig-0006">6A</a>. Among the thirty computed configurations, the ground-state configurations for HE-NMCO and LE-NMCO are depicted in Figures <a href="#anie202421089-fig-0006">6B</a> and 6 C, respectively. Subsequently, we calculated and compared the migration barriers for Na<sup>+</sup> ions based on these ground-state configurations, as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202421089-fig-0006">6D</a>. The results reveal that, owing to high-entropy engineering, the migration barriers for Na<sup>+</sup> in HE-NMCO are lower than those in LE-NMCO. Specifically, the barrier for Na<sup>+</sup> ions in proximity to both Co and Mn in HE-NMCO is notably reduced. Remarkably, the lowest migration barrier recorded is for Na<sup>+</sup> adjacent to Mg, only 0.16 eV. These findings underscore the substantial potential for superior rate capability in P2-type HE-NMCO cathode materials.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202421089-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a75c1df2-4b8e-460f-90a4-f999e9bbc1c8/anie202421089-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/a75c1df2-4b8e-460f-90a4-f999e9bbc1c8/anie202421089-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c634f4cf-39a6-450b-8a61-0aad777b6174/anie202421089-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a75c1df2-4b8e-460f-90a4-f999e9bbc1c8/anie202421089-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202421089-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202421089" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Internal stress analysis during electrochemical cycling. (A) Calculated total energies for LE-NMCO and HE-NMCO with different TM cation occupation sites. Theoretical structural models of the optimized ground-state pristine (B) LE-NMCO and (C) HE-NMCO. (D) The calculated migration barriers of Na<sup>+</sup> near to different TM cations, and the inset shows the migration pathway of Na<sup>+</sup> in LE-NMCO and HE-NMCO, respectively. (E) Calculated stress of the unit cell with different entropy configurations in LE-NMCO and HE-NMCO, respectively. (F) The simulated stress diagram of LE-NMCO and HE-NMCO, respectively.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>In addition to studying ion transport kinetics, the enhanced electrochemical behaviors of HE-NMCO are further supported by the electrochemical stress analysis presented in Figures <a href="#anie202421089-fig-0006">6E</a> and <a href="#anie202421089-fig-0006">6F</a>. To evaluate the effects of entropy-engineering on electrochemically induced stress variations, we investigated the desodiation process from <i>x</i>=0.63 to <i>x</i>=0.09 for both ground-state configurations of HE-NMCO and LE-NMCO. During this desodiation process, the unit cell of LE-NMCO exhibits substantial electrochemical stress, peaking at approximately 38 times that of its pristine state (as shown by the blue line in Figure <a href="#anie202421089-fig-0006">6E</a>). In contrast, HE-NMCO displays a considerably more moderate variation in electrochemical stress, reaching a maximum of around 8.3 times the pristine state (represented by the orange line in Figure <a href="#anie202421089-fig-0006">6E</a>). This differential in stress response correlates with changes in <i>a</i>-spacing, <i>c</i>-spacing, and <i>ΔV</i> observed in situ during electrochemical analysis. Additionally, simulations of stress distributions at the grain surfaces for both LE-NMCO and HE-NMCO reveal that mechanical and electrochemical stresses in these layered cathode materials are influenced by alkaline-ion diffusion and transition metal ion migration. During charging, the equivalent stress intensifies as Na<sup>+</sup> ions are progressively extracted, subsequently decreasing in both cathode types (Figure <a href="#anie202421089-fig-0006">6F</a>, Movies S1 and S2). Notably, stress concentration is particularly evident at high states of charge (SOC), with subsequent reductions to stress levels below those observed at high SOC. Severe stress concentrations are particularly pronounced in the LE-NMCO material, especially at a SOC of 92 %, in surface regions. The release of stress indicates that the internal stress and strain do not return reversibly to their pristine state (Movie S1). Conversely, HE-NMCO demonstrates relatively minor stress fluctuations during SOC changes, with stress concentration predominantly occurring at the shallow surface of the grains. Furthermore, the stress relief observed in HE-NMCO indicates a greater propensity to return to the pristine state (Movie S2), highlighting its superior structural reversibility and remarkable electrochemical behaviors. Electrochemical analysis of the SEM images obtained after 100 cycles (Figure S21), reveals that the HE-NMCO cathode particles maintain clear grain contours without exhibiting any signs of cracking. This observation underscores the effective mitigation of stress accumulation in the high-entropy engineered Mn-based cathode material, further validating its structural integrity and stability for prolonged cycling performance.</p>
            
            <p>In contemporary research, the high-entropy engineering approach is increasingly being applied to the cathode materials for LIBs. This includes the exploration of disordered rock-salt (DRX), Mn-LLO, and Ni-rich cathodes.<span><sup><a href="#anie202421089-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0058_R_d17635680e1517" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Nat. Mater.">58</a>-<a href="#anie202421089-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0061_R_d17635680e1517" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - Energy Environ. Sci.">61</a></sup></span> However, it is imperative to meticulously consider the content and selection of electrochemically inert elements, as their presence may adversely affect several critical performance metrics, including reversible capacity, energy density, and cycle life.<span><sup><a href="#anie202421089-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0062_R_d17635680e1520" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 62 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">62</a>, <a href="#anie202421089-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202421089-bib-0063_R_d17635680e1520" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 63 - ACS Appl. Energ. Mater.">63</a></sup></span> Consequently, in contrast to the high-entropy methodology applied to SIB cathodes, strategies such as trace elemental doping and the construction of surface high-entropy composites may prove to be more favorable for the development of advanced cathode materials for LIBs.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202421089-sec-0006">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202421089-sec-0006-title"> Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, this study presents a comprehensive investigation of a Mn-based P2-type high-entropy layered oxide cathode, specifically Na<sub>0.63</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>Co<sub>0.15</sub>Mg<sub>0.15</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>Ru<sub>0.1</sub>Cu<sub>0.05</sub>Ca<sub>0.05</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. The research effectively explores the interplay between high-entropy engineering and the mechanical and electrochemical properties of the material. The findings indicate that the HE-NMCO cathode, characterized by its multi-component composition, exhibits remarkable electrochemical performance, achieving a highly reversible capacity of 181.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> alongside an impressive rate capability, with over 83 % rate retention at a charge/discharge rate of 5 C/0.5 C. Structural and chemical analyses reveal that the high-entropy configuration plays a crucial role in mitigating structural distortion and detrimental phase transitions. This stabilization supports both the maintenance of reversible structure and the retention of capacity over extended cycling periods. Furthermore, the cathode demonstrates significant capacity for oxygen redox reactions, particularly under fully charged conditions following prolonged cycling through deep (de)desodiation processes. DFT calculations and theoretical simulations elucidate that stress concentration and variations within the high-entropy engineered P2-type cathode are effectively alleviated. Additionally, these methods demonstrate an enhancement in Na<sup>+</sup> transport kinetics facilitated by adaptable local chemistry. This research provides valuable insights into addressing the structural instability commonly associated with high-voltage operations in Mn-based layered oxide cathodes. The outcomes indicate promising avenues for achieving both high energy and power densities in sodium-layered oxide batteries, thereby advancing the field of battery technology.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202421089-sec-0007">
